16.3 Additional notes
The invariant subspace result stated as Theorem 16.1.2 is from Brown, Chevreau
and Pearcy (1979). If T is a contraction such that Ïƒ(T ) is dominating in D,
then Ïƒ(T ) contains the unit circle (Exercise 16.2.5). It was proved by Brown,
Chevreau and Pearcy (1988) that the latter condition sufï¬ces to guarantee the
existence of a non-trivial invariant subspace. A very readable proof of this re-
sult is contained in Bercovici (1990). An extension to the case of polynomially
bounded operators on Banach spaces is given in Ambrozie and MÂ¨uller (2003).
Results of Bercovici, FoiaÂ¸s and Pearcy show that the H âˆ-functional calculus of
a completely nonâ€“unitary contraction whose essential spectrum Ïƒe(T ) is dom-
inating in D allows the factorization of inï¬nite matrices with coefï¬cients in
the predual Q of H âˆ(D). It follows that contractions of this type possess an
extremely rich invariant subspace lattice. For details on this class of contrac-
tions, and many other results on the structure of the invariant subspace lattice
of contractions obtained with the Scott Brown technique, the reader is referred
to Bercovici, FoiaÂ¸s and Pearcy (1985).

17.3 Additional notes
Using methods similar to the above, K. Yan (1988) proved that each subnormal
tuple, that is, each commuting tuple S = (S1, . . . , Sn) âˆˆB(H)n for which there
is a commuting tuple N = (N1, . . . , Nn) âˆˆB(K)n of normal operators on a
larger Hilbert space K âŠƒH with Ni H âŠ‚H and Si = Ni|H, has a non-trivial
joint invariant subspace.
Let T âˆˆB(H) be arbitrary, and let M â‰¥1 be a constant. A compact set K
in C containing Ïƒ(T ) is called M-spectral for T if âˆ¥r(T )âˆ¥â‰¤M|r|K for each
rational function r with poles off K. For a subnormal operator S, its spectrum is
a spectral (= 1-spectral) set (Exercise 18.2.2). It was shown by Stampï¬‚i (1980)
that each operator T âˆˆB(H) for which the spectrum Ïƒ(T ) is an M-spectral set
possesses a non-trivial invariant subspace. Extensions of this result to the case
of operators on a Banach space are given in Prunaru (1996).

18
Invariant subspaces for subdecomposable
operators
18.1
Subnormal operators are examples of hyponormal operators. An operator
T âˆˆB(H) is hyponormal if âˆ¥T âˆ—xâˆ¥â‰¤âˆ¥T xâˆ¥for all x âˆˆH. The following gen-
eralization of Theorem 17.1.1 was obtained in Scott Brown (1987).
Theorem 18.1.1 (Brown)
Each hyponormal operator with thick spectrum has
a non-trivial invariant subspace.
â–¡
Here a compact set K in C is called thick if there is a bounded open set V in
C such that K is dominating in V . If a compact set K in C is not thick, then
R(K) = C(K) (see Brown 1987). The uniform algebra R(K) is described in
Part I, Examples 1.2(v). In particular, each subnormal operator with non-thick
spectrum is normal (Exercise 18.2.3).
To prove the above theorem, Scott Brown used a result of Putinar showing
that each hyponormal operator is the restriction of an operator with a Câˆ(C)-
functional calculus (see Eschmeier and Putinar 1996, Chapter 6.4). Since oper-
ators of the latter type are decomposable, each hyponormal operator is sub-
decomposable, that is, it is the restriction of a decomposable operator. Therefore
the next result extends Theorem 18.1.1. For the deï¬nition and the theory of
decomposable operators, see Part IV.
Theorem 18.1.2 (Eschmeier and Prunaru)
Let S âˆˆB(X) be a subdecompos-
able operator on a complex Banach space X.
(i) If Ïƒ(S) is thick, then Lat(S) is non-trivial.
(ii) If Ïƒe(S) is thick, then Lat(S) is rich.
171

172
Part III
Invariant subspaces, JÂ¨org Eschmeier
Here Lat(S) is called rich if it contains a sublattice which is isomorphic (as a
lattice) to the lattice of all closed linear subspaces of some inï¬nite-dimensional
Banach space.

ideas. First note that it sufï¬ces to prove the second part. Indeed, if Ïƒ(S) Ì¸= Ïƒe(S)
and if dim(X) > 1, then Lat(S) is non-trivial.

18.3 Additional notes
The ï¬rst applications of the Scott Brown technique to Banach-space operators
were given by Apostol (1981). Theorem 18.1.2 is from Eschmeier and Prunaru
(1990). Special cases of Theorem 18.1.2 for operators acting on quotients of
closed subspaces of â„“p (1 < p < âˆ) have been obtained by Albrecht and
Chevreau (1987). A multivariable version of Theorem 18.1.2 is contained in
EschmeierandPutinar(1996).Usingdualitymethodsonecanproveananalogue
of Theorem 18.1.2 for quotients of decomposable operators (see Eschmeier
and Prunaru 1990, Theorem 2.1). Intrinsic characterizations of subdecompos-
able operators and of quotients of decomposable operators have been given
in Albrecht and Eschmeier (1997). For instance, a continuous linear operator
S âˆˆB(X) on a Banach space X is subdecomposable if and only if it satisï¬es
Bishopâ€™s property (Î²), that is, all multiplication operators
H(U, X) â†’H(U, X),
f â†’(z âˆ’S) f
(U âŠ‚C open),
are continuous with closed range. For a discussion of property (Î²), see
Chapter 23. A proof of the ï¬rst part of Theorem 18.1.2 which does not make
use of canonical extensions and liftings was given more recently in Eschmeier
and Prunaru (2002).

19
Reï¬‚exivity of operator algebras
19.1
A subalgebra A âŠ‚B(H) is called reï¬‚exive if A = Alg Lat(A), where the space
on the right-hand side consists of all operatorsC âˆˆB(H) with Lat(A) âŠ‚Lat(C).
A single operator T âˆˆB(H), or more generally, an n-tuple T = (T1, . . . , Tn)
in B(H)n is called reï¬‚exive if the smallest WOT-closed subalgebra WT of B(H)
containing T1, . . . , Tn and the identity operator is reï¬‚exive. Equivalently, one
can ask that
Alg Lat(T ) = WT ,
where Alg Lat(T ) consists of all operators C âˆˆB(H) with Lat(T ) âŠ‚Lat(C).
Sarason proved in 1966 that each normal operator and each analytic Toeplitz
operator on H 2(T) is reï¬‚exive. In 1971 it was shown by Deddens that all
isometries are reï¬‚exive. Since all these operators are subnormal, the following
result of Olin and Thomson (1980) contains the above observations as special
cases.
Theorem 19.1.1 (Olin and Thomson)
Every subnormal operator is reï¬‚exive.
In the following we indicate some of the main steps leading to a proof of the
Olin and Thomson result. For simplicity, we shall restrict ourselves to the case
of pure subnormal operators.
Let S âˆˆB(H) be subnormal. It is easy to see that the set

It is an open question whether the invariant-subspace result formulated in
Theorem 20.1.9 remains true when the Harte spectrum Ïƒ H(T ) is replaced by the
Taylor spectrum of T . The Taylor spectrum always contains the Harte spectrum,
and it is, in many respects, the more natural joint spectrum (see Eschmeier and
Putinar 1996). The answer to this question is positive in dimension n = 2 (see
Didas 2002).

20.2 Exercises
1. Use Theorem 20.1.5 and the ball analogue of Corollary 15.1.4 to prove that,
if Âµ âˆˆM(âˆ‚B) is a Henkin measure, then every measure Î½ âˆˆM(âˆ‚B) with
Î½ â‰ªÂµ is a Henkin measure. This result is usually referred to as Henkinâ€™s
theorem.
2. Complete the proof of Theorem 20.1.2 as outlined in this chapter.
3. Let & : A(B) â†’B(H) be a contractive algebra homomorphism. Show that
there is at most one pair (&1, &2) consisting of contractive algebra homo-
morphisms &i : A(B) â†’B(H) with & = &1 + &2 such that &1 has a rep-
resenting family of measures that are absolutely continuous, and &2 has a
representing family of measures that are singular on âˆ‚B.
4. Let & : A(B) â†’B(H) be a continuous algebra homomorphism. Show that
there is an extension of & to a weak-âˆ—continuous algebra homomorphism
 : H âˆ(B) â†’B(H) if and only if & has a representing family of measures
on âˆ‚B that are absolutely continuous, or equivalently, if each representing
family of measures for & on âˆ‚B consists of absolutely continuous measures.
5. Use Rudin (1980, Theorem 6.5.4) to prove Theorem 20.1.5.
6. Let T âˆˆB(H)n be a spherical contraction. Show that there is a unique or-
thogonal decomposition H = H0 âŠ•H1 with reducing subspaces H0, H1 for
T such that T |H0 is completely non-unitary and T |H1 is a spherical unitary.
20.3 Additional notes
Henkinâ€™s theorem and the Coleâ€“Range theorem imply that on âˆ‚B the set of
all Henkin measures coincides with the band of measures generated by the
set M0 of all measures that represent the point evaluation at 0 on A(B). Al-
though Henkinâ€™s theorem fails on the unit polydisc Dn, one can still show (see
Eschmeier 2001a) that the band of measures generated by the set M0(Tn) of
all measures on Tn that represent the point evaluation at 0 on A(Dn) is the
largest band in M(Tn) consisting entirely of Henkin measures. This result is
used in Eschmeier (2001a, 2001b) to show that Theorem 20.1.9 remains true
if the open unit ball is replaced by the open unit polydisc, and to prove an
analogue of Theorem 20.1.10 on Dn. Since by Andoâ€™s dilation theorem every
pair T = (T1, T2) of commuting contractions has a dilation to a commuting
pair U = (U1, U2) of unitary operators, we obtain in particular that every pair
T = (T1, T2) of commuting contractions with dominating Harte spectrum in
D2 has a non-trivial joint invariant subspace. It is an open question whether
the Harte spectrum can be replaced by the Taylor spectrum in this result. This
question has a positive answer, even in arbitrary dimension n, if the tuple T is

192
Part III
Invariant subspaces, JÂ¨org Eschmeier
supposed to consist of doubly commuting contractions (see Albrecht and Ptak
1998).
Theorem 20.1.10 remains true if the condition that T possesses an isometric
weak-âˆ—continuous H âˆ(B)-functional calculus is replaced by the hypothesis
that the Taylor spectrum of T is dominating in B (see Eschmeier 1999). Reï¬‚ex-
ivity results for spherical contractions that extend the above mentioned results
for subnormal spherical contractions can be found in Eschmeier (2001c). In
Didas (2002), many of the above results are shown to hold for commuting tu-
ples T âˆˆB(H)n which possess an isometric, weak-âˆ—continuous H âˆ-functional
calculus  : H âˆ(D) â†’B(H) and a normal boundary dilation over a strictly
pseudoconvex domain D in Cn, or more generally, a strictly pseudoconvex open
set D in a Stein submanifold X of Cn. In particular, every subnormal n-tuple
with dominating Taylor spectrum in D, or with isometric weak-âˆ—continuous
H âˆ(D)-functional calculus, is shown to be reï¬‚exive.
By a result of Bercovici (1994) at least each commuting family of isometries
on a Hilbert space is reï¬‚exive. Azoff and Ptak (1995) extended this result to the
case of jointly quasinormal tuples. The question whether each spherical isome-
try is reï¬‚exive seems still to be open. Some partial results have been obtained in
MÂ¨uller and Ptak (1999) and Eschmeier (2001c). The general question, whether
every subnormal tuple S âˆˆB(H)n on a Hilbert space is reï¬‚exive, is one of the
major open questions in this area at the time of this writing.

21
Basic notions from operator theory
21.1 Introduction
This part of the book is intended as an invitation to the subject of local spectral
theory. It contains the basics and some indications of the way the subject has
developed. I would like to thank Garth Dales and Michael Neumann for their
numerous good comments and suggestions. The entire story of the fascinating
subject that Chapters 21â€“25 deal with may be found in Laursen and Neumann
(2000), and I hope that after having been through these chapters you will want
to go for more in that book, which also contains a full bibliography.
The phrase local spectral theory carries many connotations. Among the ones
that are appropriate here you should expect to ï¬nd concepts such as spectral
subspaces, that is, invariant subspaces on which the restricted operator has
a spectrum consisting of a chunk of the original spectrum. The archetypal con-
ceptual framework is provided by the spectral theorem for normal operators
on a Hilbert space, which speciï¬es how this decomposition of the underly-
ing space and of the spectrum is supposed to look. Another similar exam-
ple is provided by the spectral theorem for compact operators on a Banach
space.
Both of these examples may be traced back to what is often a high point
of a ï¬rst course in linear algebra, namely a result on diagonalizing symmetric
matrices such as the following. (A symmetric matrix [ai j] satisï¬es the relations
ai j = a ji for all i, j, while for a symmetric operator T on a ï¬nite-dimensional,
real inner-product space V with inner product [Â·, Â·], it is true that we have
[T x, y] = [x, T y] for all x, y âˆˆV .)
H. G. Dales, P. Aiena, J. Eschmeier, K. B. Laursen, and G. A. Willis, Introduction to Banach
Algebras, Operators, and Harmonic Analysis. Published by Cambridge University Press.
câƒCambridge University Press 2003.

Local spectral theory, Kjeld Bagger Laursen
Theorem 21.1.1
A linear operator T : V â†’V, where V is a ï¬nite-
dimensional real inner-product space, is symmetric if and only if V has an
orthonormal basis consisting of eigenvectors for T.
â–¡

operators. To appreciate this from our chosen vantage point we need to know
what a spectral measure is. An operator N âˆˆB(H) is normal when it satisï¬es

It is in this light that the modern deï¬nition of a decomposable operator should
be viewed.
Throughout these Chapters 21â€“25, we shall be considering complex Banach
spaces (as well as Banach algebras), and it will be assumed that, unless some-
thing else is explicitly stipulated, the term X refers to a complex Banach space,
and the term A refers to a commutative complex Banach algebra.
Deï¬nition 21.1.4
A bounded linear operator T : X â†’X is decomposable if,
for every open cover {U, V } of C, there are closed T -invariant subspaces Y, Z
of X for which:
r X = Y + Z;
r Ïƒ(T |Y) âŠ†U and Ïƒ(T |Z) âŠ†V.
Chapter 22 will look at examples in more detail (but it is an easy consequence
of Theorem 21.1.3 that normal operators are decomposable â€“ see Exercise
21.3.1). Here we shall concentrate on introducing the basic concepts.
If U âŠ†C is open, then H(U) denotes the algebra (pointwise operations) of
analytic functions deï¬ned on U. We give this algebra the topology of locally
uniform convergence, that is, of uniform convergence on all compact subsets of
U. It may be shown that this topology is locally convex and that it is induced by
a complete and translation-invariant metric; thus H(U) is a FrÂ´echet algebra (see
Part I, Examples 1.2 (x)). Among the elements of H(U) we ï¬nd the constant
function 1 and the identity function Z.
The analytic functional calculus is deï¬ned in Chapter 4. We recall it this
way: if T âˆˆB(X) has spectrum Ïƒ(T ) and U is an open neighbourhood of Ïƒ(T )
on which the analytic function f is deï¬ned, then the map

There are connections between functional calculi and decomposability, al-
though in the case of the analytic functional calculus this connection is limited
to the situations in which the spectrum is disconnected. To display an extreme
case: this calculus may be used to show that, if Ïƒ(T ) is totally disconnected,
then T is decomposable (Exercise 21.3.2). (Recall from general topology that
a topological space is said to be totally disconnected if none of its connected
subsets contains more than one point.) The reason the argument goes through is

A generalized scalar operator is decomposable. This is a consequence of the
fact that in Câˆ(C) we have partitions of unity (cf. Exercise 21.3.3).
21.2 The properties (Î²) and (Î´)
The main goal for the rest of this chapter is to introduce two seemingly merely
technical conditions, and to relate them to decomposability. Our aim is Theorem
21.2.8, which shows that these two conditions together are equivalent to de-
composability. It will become clear in the course of the next few chapters that
both conditions have some truly astounding relations, which go well beyond

this ï¬rst result. Both properties (which are called properties (Î²) and (Î´)) go

Deï¬nition 21.2.1

Deï¬nition 21.2.2

That decomposability implies (Î´) is immediate: if C = U âˆªV and Y, Z are

21.3 Exercises
1. Use Theorem 21.1.3 to show that normal operators are decomposable.
2. Show that, if Ïƒ(T ) is totally disconnected, then T is decomposable.
3. Show that a generalized scalar operator is decomposable Hint: in Câˆ(C) we
have partitions of unity.
4. Show that an operator T has (Î²) if and only if TU is injective and has closed
range, for every open set U in C.
5. Find an example of a bounded linear operator which has SVEP, but not (Î²).
6. Use Liouvilleâ€™s theorem to show that XT (âˆ…) = {0}.
7. Prove that the map T â™®introduced after Deï¬nition 21.2.4 is continuous.
8. Showthat,sinceXT (F) = TC\F H(C \ F, X) âˆ©X,XT (F)isclosedprovided
that T has (Î²). (cf. the proof of Theorem 21.2.8.)
9. Suppose that XT (F) is closed. Show that Ïƒ(T |XT (F)) âŠ†F. Hint: use the
open mapping theorem.
21.4 Additional notes
Decomposability may be described entirely in terms of spectral capacities
(subspace-valued functions from the Borel sets of C which behave pretty much
like the ranges of spectral projections, cf. Deï¬nition 21.1.2), which brings it
conceptually even closer to the spectral theorem. The class of decomposable
operators is but one of the natural extensions based in the spectral theorem. We
shall deï¬ne a couple of them in the next chapter, but there are others: spectral
and scalar operators, going back to Dunford, as well as various strengthenings
of the decomposability requirement. Chapter 1 of Laursen and Neumann (2000)
tells more. Of course, Hilbert space operator theory has a history and dynamics

21
Basic notions from operator theory
211
of its own, displayed by the appearance of several classes containing the normal
ones, such as sub- or hyponormal operators.
The history of decomposability is quite interesting. The subject has generated
many classes of operators, as well as a conceptual evolution, for instance of the
very deï¬nition of decomposability, which was quite complicated when FoiaÂ¸s
ï¬rst proposed it, but now, thanks to Albrecht and Lange, reads very simply.
Among the leading classical practitioners of this branch of operator theory we
ï¬nd people such as Dunford, Bishop, FoiaÂ¸s, Vasilescu, Frunza and Albrecht,
while the more recent developments have been strongly inï¬‚uenced by, inter alia,
Albrecht, Lange, Eschmeier and Putinar. Chapter 1 of Laursen and Neumann
(2000) contains an account of all this.
In Gleason (1962), it was shown that Proposition 21.2.5 actually holds for
arbitrary open subsets U of C. The proof of this general version is considerably
more involved, and such a high level of generality is not needed in the present
work.

22
Classes of decomposable operators
22.1 Local properties of operators
In this chapter we shall take a closer look at super-decomposable and at gen-

operators with (Î²), namely all isometries.
The companion concept of the glocal subspace is the local analytic spectral
subspace. Here is ï¬rst the deï¬nition of local spectrum.

It follows directly from the deï¬nition that XT (FÎ±) = XT (FÎ±) for arbi-

22.2 Super-decomposable operators
Here is our ï¬rst class of examples of decomposable operators. The super-
decomposable operators are based on the idea of operator-valued partitions
of unity. More speciï¬cally, we are considering a class of operators for which
the condition of decomposability is implemented by certain operator ranges.
Deï¬nition 22.2.1
An operator T âˆˆB(X) is super-decomposable if, for every
open cover of C by two subsets U and V, there is an operator R âˆˆB(X) which

Ïƒ(T | (I âˆ’R)X) âŠ†V .
This deï¬nition makes sense because, by the condition R T = T R, the spaces
RX and (I âˆ’R)X are T -invariant. It is obvious that super-decomposable
operators are decomposable.

It is interesting that for many super-decomposable operators it is possible to

This is important in applications of local spectral theory to automatic continuity

(dating back to Johnson and Sinclair (1969) which are called the algebraic
spectral subspaces.

shows that E T (F) is the largest linear subspace with respect to this surjectivity
condition.

Duality theory
In this chapter we shall explore the surprising relations between decomposabil-
ity and properties (Î²) and (Î´).
23.1 Duality between (Î²) and (Î´)
We have already seen, in Theorem 21.2.8, that T is decomposable if and only if it
has both (Î²) and (Î´). But the main reason for emphasizing these two properties
is not just that they together describe decomposability â€“ surely (Î²), in particular,
is too technical, and too non-intuitive, to gain fame just for that! Their main
conceptual raison dâ€™Ë†etre is that they possess a remarkable dual relationship: an
operator will have one of them (either one!) precisely when its adjoint operator
has the other one. This is the signiï¬cant conclusion that is provided by the
duality theory. (You should note that the adjoint operator is called the dual
operator in Part I.)
The duality theory for operators that we are talking about here goes back to
Errett Bishopâ€™s PhD thesis, published in Bishop (1959), where he developed a
spectral theory for an arbitrary bounded linear operator on a reï¬‚exive Banach
space. Bishop called his development a duality theory, because the operator
and its adjoint are involved. In Bishop (1959) we can see, in more or less fully
developed form, much of what are now basic tools and concepts of the ï¬eld,
such as the glocal spectral subspaces, and conditions (Î²) and (Î´). There was even
a precursor of decomposability (which Bishop called duality theory of type 3).
Theorem 21.2.8 (that T is decomposable if and only if it has both (Î²) and
(Î´)) was of course not available to Bishop. But he did show that, if both T and
T â€² satisfy (Î²), then T has a duality theory of type 3, that is, T is decomposable.
This result was established without Bishopâ€™s reï¬‚exivity condition by FrunzË˜a
(1971). Bishop also showed that, if T â€² has (Î²), then T has (Î´). This was a result
that rather glaringly used reï¬‚exivity, because it showed that (T â€²)â€² has (Î´).
226

23
Duality theory
227
The full duality theory owes its spectacular completion to Albrecht and
Eschmeier (1997). It contains the following results. They are not complicated
to state, but a lot of ingenuity lies behind their proofs. You will ï¬nd reasonably
accessible proofs in Laursen and Neumann (2000, Chapter 2).
Theorem 23.1.1
A continuous linear operator on a Banach space has (Î´) if
and only if it is a quotient of a decomposable operator with respect to a closed
invariant subspace.
â–¡
Theorem 23.1.2
A continuous linear operator on a Banach space has (Î²) if
and only if it is a restriction of a decomposable operator to a closed invariant
subspace.
â–¡
Incidentally, the latter theorem explains why operators with (Î²) are often
called sub-decomposable operators.
In both theorems, the if parts are easy enough. The approach to proving, say,
Theorem 23.1.2 may be explained in fairly non-technical terms as follows. At
its root is a pair of 3-space lemmas both of which may be combined into the
following statement. The proof for the (Î²) case is easy, the (Î´) case is not.

Preservation of spectra and index
It is well known that, if two linear operators on a ï¬nite-dimensional space are
similar, then their spectra are equal, and that this equality extends to distin-
guished parts of the spectra, such as the point spectra, etc. Here we consider
natural extensions of this situation, where similarity is replaced by weaker
conditions, and where concepts and techniques from local spectral theory are
used.
24.1 An intertwining result
We will concentrate on proving the following result.
Theorem 24.1.1
Suppose that S âˆˆB(Y) and T âˆˆB(X) both have property
(Î²) and that A âˆˆB(X, Y) and B âˆˆB(Y, X) are operators with dense ranges
for which SA = AT and T B = BS. Then Ïƒ(T ) = Ïƒ(S), Ïƒe(T ) = Ïƒe(S) and
ind(T âˆ’Î») = ind(S âˆ’Î») for all Î» âˆˆÏe(T ).
First we shall explain the terms. Recall that an operator T âˆˆB(X) is a
Fredholm operator (or just Fredholm) if its null space ker T is of ï¬nite di-
mension and its range T X is of ï¬nite codimension in X; the range is then
automatically closed (Exercise 24.3.1). The essential spectrum Ïƒe(T ) of T is
the set of complex numbers Î» for which T âˆ’Î» is not Fredholm (often expressed
this way: Î» is not a Fredholm point for T .) Fredholm theory is developed more
fully in Part V.
A relation such as SA = AT is described by saying that A intertwines the
pair (S, T ). If A is a bijection, then S and T are similar, and A is a similarity. As
we mentioned, a similarity preserves spectra, and even the ï¬ner structure of the
spectra (point spectrum, approximate point spectrum, Fredholm points, etc.).
Here we shall see what happens if the conditions on the intertwining operator
230

24
Preservation of spectra and index
231
are weakened, for example to that of being a quasi-afï¬nity, that is, an injective
map with dense range, or even if both (S, T ) and (T, S) are intertwined by
quasi-afï¬nities; in this latter case the operators S and T are said to be quasi-
similar. Note, incidentally, that the answer provided by Theorem 24.1.1 is made
on intertwining assumptions that are slightly weaker than quasi-similarity.
The index of a Fredholm operator T âˆˆB(X) is
ind(T ) := dim ker T âˆ’dim(X/T X) .
Here are a few basic results from classical Fredholm theory: an operator
T âˆˆB(X)isFredholmifandonlyifitsadjointoperator T â€² âˆˆB(Xâ€²)isFredholm;
in fact, since the dimension of the null space of one equals the codimension of
the range of the other, we see that
ind(T â€²) = âˆ’ind(T ) .
The product of two Fredholm operators is again Fredholm. In particular,
every power of a Fredholm operator is Fredholm. The index theorem says that
the index is a homomorphism, that is,
ind(T S) = ind(T ) + ind(S)
for all Fredholm operators T, S âˆˆB(X). Since invertible operators are
Fredholm operators of index zero, it follows that the Fredholm property as
well as the index are preserved under similarity.
Does this hold for quasi-similarities also?
Example 24.1.2
(Fialkow (1977), here reproduced from Laursen and
Neumann 2000, Example 3.7.12). Let T be the unilateral right shift on â„“2(N),
and let S be the unilateral weighted right shift on â„“2(N) with weight sequence
(1/(n + 1))nâˆˆN. Then T has (Î²), Ïƒ(T ) = D, and Ïƒe(T ) = T, while S is compact
with Ïƒ(S) = Ïƒe(S) = {0}. Thus Ïƒe(T ) and Ïƒe(S) are disjoint. However, the pair
(S, T ) is intertwined by a quasi-afï¬nity, namely the multiplication operator A
on â„“2(N) given by
Ax := (xn/n!)nâˆˆN
for all x = (xn)nâˆˆN âˆˆâ„“2(N).
It is easy to see that A is injective, has dense range, and that SA = A T.

The proof of Theorem 24.1.1 is relatively elementary, but lengthy. It begins
by using an observation about restrictions of Fredholm operators to certain
invariant subspaces.
Proposition 24.1.4
Suppose that T âˆˆB(X), and that Z âŠ†X is a T -invariant
closed linear subspace of ï¬nite codimension in X. Then T is a Fredholm op-
erator on X if and only if T | Z is a Fredholm operator on Z. Moreover, if the
two operators are both Fredholm, they have the same index.
Proof Pick a projection P âˆˆB(X) with range Z, and observe that the space
(I âˆ’P)X = ker P is ï¬nite-dimensional. Since T (I âˆ’P) is of ï¬nite-rank,
and T equals T P + T (I âˆ’P), it follows that T and T P are Fredholm
simultaneously, and that, in this case, ind(T ) = ind(T P). Moreover, since an
easy calculation will reveal that we have ker(T P) = ker(T | Z) âŠ•ker P and
(T P)(X) = (T | Z)(Z),we conclude that T P and T | Z also are Fredholm si-
multaneously and that, when this happens, they will have the same index.

24.2 Automatic continuity
Automatic continuity theory for homomorphisms and derivations is discussed
in Part I, Chapter 5. Just to give an impression of the kind of automatic con-
tinuity results that may be established on the basis of local spectral theory, I
quote one theorem. There is not sufï¬cient space here to dwell on how it is
proved, but it and others like it may be found in Laursen and Neumann (2000,
Chapter 5). However, signiï¬cant in the arguments is the simple observation that
the algebraic spectral subspaces, discussed immediately after Example 22.2.5,
have the property (analogous to what is true for both the local and the glocal
spectral subspaces and continuous , in the notation of this next theorem) that
ET (F) âŠ†ES(F), when S = T.
Theorem 24.2.1
Suppose that T âˆˆB(X) is decomposable, while S âˆˆB(Y)
is generalized scalar. Suppose also that  : X â†’Y is a linear transformation
for which S = T. Then there is a ï¬nite set  âŠ‚C such that |XT (F) is
continuous for every closed F âŠ†C which is disjoint from . Moreover, there
is a non-zero polynomial p, all of whose roots are eigenvalues of S, such that
the composition p(S) : X â†’Y is continuous. In particular,  is continuous
if S has no eigenvalues.
â–¡
The ï¬nite set  is called the singularity set. The condition on T may be
relaxed to that of assuming (Î´), while that on S could be replaced by super-
decomposable and ES(âˆ…) = {0}, and even more general conditions. For other
results on automatic continuity, see Part I, Chapter 5.

24.4 Additional notes
The issue of spectral overlaps has received a lot of attention. For general Banach
spaces, the classical Lumer and Rosenblum (1959) result says that two operators

240
Part IV
Local spectral theory, Kjeld Bagger Laursen
(S, T ) intertwined by a non-zero intertwiner must have overlapping spectra, and
this result was reï¬ned by Davis and Rosenthal (1974) to Ïƒsu(T ) âˆ©Ïƒap(S) Ì¸= âˆ….
Both of these results are provable, in even greater generality, by local spectral
methods. Laursen and Neumann (2000, Chapter 3) has details, and work on this
may also be found in Laursen and Neumann (1994): it is not even necessary to
suppose that SA = AT . There are ways of saying how close parts of the two
spectra must be in terms of the commutator SA âˆ’AT.
Fialkow (1977) also showed that, in contrast to Example 24.1.2, the essential
spectra of arbitrary quasi-similar operators on a separable Hilbert space always
have non-empty intersection. Moreover, Herrero (1988) showed that, for every
pair of quasi-similar operators T âˆˆB(X) and S âˆˆB(Y) on the Banach spaces
X and Y, each connected component of Ïƒe(T ) touches Ïƒe(S), and vice versa.
This result now has an attractive sheaf-theoretic proof (see Eschmeier 2000).
In this chapter we presented a proof of the (Î²) part of Theorem 24.1.1.
This proof is quite elementary compared with previous ones. Theorem 24.1.1
has a background that may be traced to a problem from Clary (1975): having
established the equality of the spectra for quasi-similar hyponormal opera-
tors (hyponormal T means that the commutator T âˆ—T âˆ’T T âˆ—â‰¥0), Clary asked
whether this extends to the essential spectra. This problem received consider-
able attention. The ï¬nal solution, due to Putinar (1992) and, independently, to
Yang (1993), revealed that the assumption of hyponormal is not really needed:
all that matters is that hyponormal operators have (Î²). But generally, of course,
Hilbert space operators have their own theory.

25
Multipliers on commutative Banach algebras
25.1 Multipliers
In this chapter I want to give an impression of what sort of information becomes
available via local spectral theory when it is applied to a particular class of op-
erators, namely the multipliers on a commutative Banach algebra. Throughout
this chapter, the letter A will denote a commutative, complex Banach algebra.
Deï¬nition 25.1.1
A linear map T : A â†’A is a multiplier if aT (b) = T (a)b
for all a, b âˆˆA. The set of multipliers on A is denoted by M(A).
The most obvious example, given A, is the multiplication operator La in-
duced by a ï¬xed element a âˆˆA, that is, the operator La(b) := ab for all b âˆˆA.
If A has a unit e (A is unital) then every multiplier T is a multiplication operator.
In this case T = LT (e) (Exercise 25.3.2).
If the map a â†’La : A â†’B(A) is injective (faithful), then A is said to be
a faithful algebra. Every unital algebra is faithful, as is every semisimple, and
also every semiprime algebra â€“ the latter term means, in our commutative case,
that the algebra contains no non-zero nilpotent elements.

25.2 The hull-kernel topology
The second main result of this chapter is Theorem 25.2.2. To appreciate all of it,
it is necessary to realize that, in addition to the Gelfand topology, there is another
topology on the character space A of A. And it is also worth knowing that
the condition of continuity of the Gelfand transform of a given algebra element
with respect to this other topology, which is the main assumption of Theorem
25.2.2, is really a decomposability condition in disguise: in Proposition 25.2.5
it is established that, if A is faithful and if T âˆˆM(A) is a multiplier with (Î´),
then the restriction T | A is hk-continuous. This of course applies in particular
to a multiplication operator.
The topology that we are referring to is introduced in the next proposition,
stated here without proof; it is called the hull-kernel topology, or hk-topology,
of A. This topology is deï¬ned via speciï¬cation of the closure of any given
subset of the character space. This involves the following steps, already given
as Exercise 3.6.1 in Part I. For a set S âŠ†A, the kernel of S is the ideal of A
deï¬ned as
k(S) := {a âˆˆA : Ï•(a) = 0 for all Ï• âˆˆS} ,
and, symmetrically, for an ideal J âŠ†A, the hull of J in A is
h(J) := {Ï• âˆˆA : Ï•(a) = 0 for all a âˆˆJ} .
For an arbitrary subset S of A, we then deï¬ne the hk-closure of S by
hk(S) := h(k(S)) = {Ï• âˆˆA : a(Ï•) = 0 for all a âˆˆA with a â‰¡0 on S} .
Proposition 25.2.1
Let A be a commutative complex algebra. The sets of the
form hk(S), where S ranges over the subsets of the character space A, are the
closed sets of a topology, the hk-topology of A. This topology has the following
properties:
(i) for any S âŠ†A, the closure of S in the hk-topology is precisely hk(S);
(ii) the hk-topology is coarser than (or equal to) the Gelfand topology.
â–¡
As noted in Part I, these two topologies coincide exactly when A is regular
(regular: whenever F is a Gelfand closed subset of A and Ï• âˆˆA\F then
there is a âˆˆA for which a vanishes on F, but not at Ï•).
With these basic facts listed, we can state the theorem.
Theorem 25.2.2
Suppose that A is semisimple and that a âˆˆA is an element
for which a is hk-continuous on A. Then, for an arbitrary homomorphism

Corollary 25.2.6
Suppose that A is semisimple. Then A is regular if and only
if, for every a âˆˆA, the multiplication operator La is decomposable.
Proof If A is regular then, by the remark immediately after Proposition 25.2.1,
a is hk-continuous for every a âˆˆA.
Conversely, if every La is decomposable then Proposition 25.2.5 implies that
a is hk-continuous for every a âˆˆA. But the Gelfand topology is, by deï¬nition,
the coarsest topology on A for which all these functions are continuous. Con-
sequently, the hk-topology coincides with the Gelfand topology on A. By the
remark after Proposition 25.2.1, this establishes the regularity of A.

25.3 Exercises
1. Show that a multiplier on a faithful commutative Banach algebra is contin-
uous.
2. Show that if A is unital then every multiplier T is a multiplication operator,
by showing that in this case T = LT (e).
3. Show that, if A has approximate units, that is, for every a âˆˆA and every
Îµ > 0 there is u âˆˆA such that âˆ¥a âˆ’uaâˆ¥< Îµ, then A is faithful.

on 
.
5. Let A be faithful. Prove that the multiplier algebra M(A) is a closed, com-
mutative, unital, full subalgebra of B(A) and contains A as an ideal.
6. Show that hkM(A)(A) = h({0}) = M(A), hence that A is hk-dense in
M(A).
7. Show that if C is a commutative Banach algebra, if c âˆˆC, if c is hk-
continuous on C, and if B is any commutative algebra that contains C
as a subalgebra, thenc is hk-continuous on B.
25.4 Additional notes
Concerning examples of non-degenerate modules, it is easily seen that X is non-
degenerate and satisï¬es AX = X whenever the left A-module X is irreducible;
see Proposition 24.4 of Bonsall and Duncan (1973).
The best results about decomposability of multipliers are obtained when
certain vanishing-at-inï¬nity conditions are imposed.
Deï¬nition 25.4.1
Suppose that A is semisimple and has multiplier algebra
M(A), and character space A (respectively, M(A)). Then
M0(A) := {T âˆˆM(A) : T |A vanishes at inï¬nity on A âŠ†M(A)}
and

In the simple case of A = C(

space, it is easy to picture these two ideals. Evidently, we always have
A âŠ†M00(A) âŠ†M0(A) âŠ†M(A) ,

and in general all these containments are strict; notably, this holds when
A = L1(G) is the group algebra for a locally compact, non-discrete, abelian
group.
The next result, and its proof, may be found in Laursen and Neumann (2000,
Theorem 4.5.4).
Theorem 25.4.2
Let A be semisimple. For T âˆˆM0(A), the following are
equivalent:
(a) T is super-decomposable on A;
(b) T is decomposable on A;
(c) T has (Î´);

(f) multiplication by T is decomposable on M(A).

exactly when T âˆˆM00(A).

It is interesting that the above theorem establishes a certain kind of universal-
ity of generalized scalar operators: every bounded linear operator on a Banach
space is a restriction of a quotient of a generalized scalar operator. However,
our main concern is expressed in the next result, which is now simple. Its proof
follows by applying the 3-space lemma, Lemma 23.1.3, for (Î²).
Theorem A.5
A continuous linear operator on a Banach space has (Î²) if and
only if it is similar to the restriction of a decomposable operator to one of its
closed invariant subspaces. Moreover, if T is an operator with (Î²), then the
operator S, in the functional model of Theorem A.4 for T, is a decomposable
extension of T .

Finally, here are the detailed duality statements. Evidently, it is an immediate

â€² has (Î´), then T has (Î²).

â€² has (Î²), then T has (Î´).

23.1.1, T â€² has (Î´). Exercise: ï¬ll in the details.
(ii) and (iii): exercises.

Part V
Single-valued extension property and Fredholm
theory
PIETRO AIENA
Universit`a degli Studi, Palermo, Italy


26
Semi-regular operators
Let T be a bounded operator on a Banach space X. It has been observed in
Chapter 21 that, if the spectrum Ïƒ(T ) is totally disconnected, then T is decom-
posable. In particular, every compact operator K is decomposable, because
Ïƒ(K) is either ï¬nite or a sequence which converges to 0. The classical Rieszâ€“
Schauder theory for compact operators shows that, for every Î» Ì¸= 0, the space
Î»I âˆ’T has a ï¬nite-dimensional kernel and a ï¬nite-codimensional range, that
is, Î»I âˆ’T is a Fredholm operator, see Part I, Theorem 2.2.5 or Heuser (1982).
Similarly, the class of normal operators on a Hilbert space consists of decom-
posable operators (see Part IV, Chapter 21), and has many remarkable properties
from the point of view of Fredholm theory. For instance, for normal operators
some of the spectra originating from Fredholm theory coincide, as in the case
of compact operators.
A natural question is to what extent the results which hold for compact and
normal operators in Fredholm theory may be extended to decomposable oper-
ators. In this part of the book we shall give some answers to this question, and
establish important connections between Fredholm theory and the single-valued
extension property, a property which plays a leading role in the investigation
of decomposable operators.
26.1 Deï¬nitions
Among the various concepts of regularity originating from Fredholm theory, the
concept of semi-regularity, which will be introduced in this chapter, seems to
be the most appropriate to investigate some important aspects of local spectral

Algebras, Operators, and Harmonic Analysis. Published by Cambridge University Press.
câƒCambridge University Press 2003.

Recall that every subspace M of a vector space X admits at least one algebraic

Lemma 26.1.11
Suppose that T âˆˆB(X), where X is a Banach space, and that
T has closed range T (X). For each (not necessarily closed) subspace Y âŠ†X,
T (Y) is closed whenever Y + ker T is closed.
Proof Let x be the equivalence class x + ker T in the quotient space X/ker T ,
and denote by T : X/ker T â†’X the canonical injection which is deï¬ned by
T (x) := T x, where x âˆˆx. By assumption, T (X) is closed, so that T has a
bounded inverse T
âˆ’1 : T (X) â†’X/ker T . Let Y := {y : y âˆˆY}. It is evident
that T (Y) = T (Y) is the inverse image of Y under the continuous map T
âˆ’1.
Thus T (Y) is closed whenever Y is closed.
It remains to show that Y is closed whenever Y + ker T is closed. Let (xn) be
a sequence of Y which converges to x âˆˆX/ker T . Then there exists a sequence
(xn) with xn âˆˆxn such that dist(xn âˆ’x, ker T ) converges to zero, and so there
exists a sequence (zn) âŠ‚ker T with xn âˆ’x âˆ’zn â†’0 as n â†’âˆ. Evidently,
the sequence (xn âˆ’zn) âŠ‚Y + ker T converges to x, and since, by assumption,
Y + ker T is closed, we conclude that x âˆˆY + ker T . This implies x âˆˆY, and
hence Y is closed.

26.2 The Kato spectrum
Among the many concepts dealt with in Katoâ€™s extensive treatment of perturba-
tion theory (Kato 1958), there is a very important part of the spectrum deï¬ned
as follows.
Deï¬nition 26.2.1
Let X be a Banach space, and let T âˆˆB(X). The Kato
resolvent set of T is
ÏK(T ) := {Î» âˆˆC : Î»I âˆ’T is semi-regular}.
The Kato spectrum of T is the set ÏƒK(T ) := C \ ÏK(T ) .
It is clear that ÏƒK(T ) âŠ†Ïƒ(T ) and Ï(T ) âŠ†ÏK(T ). Later we shall prove that
ÏƒK(T ) is a non-empty, compact subset of C.
Recall that an operator T âˆˆB(X) is bounded below if T is injective and has
closed range. This is equivalent to saying that there exists K > 0 such that
âˆ¥T xâˆ¥â‰¥Kâˆ¥xâˆ¥for all x âˆˆX. Clearly, if T is bounded below or surjective, then
T is semi-regular. A standard result of duality theory shows that T is bounded
below (respectively, surjective) if and only if T â€² is surjective (respectively,
bounded below).

26.4 Additional notes
The concept of semi-regularity of an operator T âˆˆB(X), where X is a Banach
space, arose from the treatment of perturbation theory due to Kato (1958), even
if originally these operators were not given this name. Subsequently, this class
of operators has received a lot of attention from several other authors (see, for
instance, Mbekhta 1987, 1990; Mbekhta and Ouahab 1994; Schmoeger 1990;
MÂ¨uller 1994). The Kato spectrum, also known as the Apostol spectrum in the
literature, was ï¬rst introduced by Apostol (1984) for Hilbert space operators
and was deï¬ned as the set of all complex Î» such that either Î»I âˆ’T is not closed
or Î» is a discontinuity point for the function Î» â†’(Î»I âˆ’T )âˆ’1. Subsequently,
the spectrum ÏƒK(T ) has been studied by different authors in the more general
framework of Banach spaces.
The local constancy of the analytic core on the components of the Kato
resolvent has been established by several authors (see, for instance, FÂ¨orster
1966; Â´O SearcÂ´oid and West 1989), but the methods adopted in this notes are
inspired by the paper of Mbekhta and Ouahab (1994). A different approach to
this result may be found in Section 3.7 of Laursen and Neumann (2000). The
section on the quasi-nilpotent part of an operator is modelled after Mbekhta
(1990).
The concept of algebraic core of an operator has been introduced by Saphar
(1964), while the analytical core has been introduced by VrbovÂ´a (1973) and,
subsequently, studied by Mbekhta (1990), Mbekhta and Ouahab (1994). Lemma
26.1.9 and Proposition 26.1.10 are taken from Aiena and Monsalve (2000).
Proposition 26.1.12 is due to Schmoeger (1990), while the subsequent part,
except Lemma 26.2.4 due to Kato (1958), can be found in Mbekhta (1990).
Theorem 26.2.11 is due to Schmoeger (1990), who also showed that a spectral
mapping theorem holds for ÏƒK(T ).

27
The single-valued extension property
The basic role of the single-valued extension property (SVEP) arises in the
spectral decomposition theory, since every decomposable operator T enjoys
this property, as does its dual T â€². Indeed, in part IV, Chapter 21 it has
been shown that the decomposability of an operator may be viewed as the
union of two properties, the so-called Bishopâ€™s property (Î²) and the prop-
erty (Î´). Property (Î²) for T implies the SVEP for T (see part IV, Chapter
21) and, as observed in part IV, Chapter 23, properties (Î²) and (Î´) have a
complete duality, so that, if T has (Î´), then the dual T â€² has (Î²) and therefore
SVEP.
The main goal of this chapter is to investigate in detail a localized version of
SVEP. First we shall show that local spectral theory provides a suitable frame
for some characterizations of the analytical core and of the quasi-nilpotent part.
Then we shall use these characterizations to describe the localized SVEP by
means of a variety of conditions that involve the analytical core and the quasi-
nilpotent part of an operator, as well as the generalized range and the generalized
kernel.
27.1 The SVEP at a point
To explain the role of SVEP in local spectral theory we begin with some pre-
liminary and well-known facts from operator theory.

27.4 Additional notes
The single-valued extension property appeared ï¬rst in Dunford (1952, 1954),
and then received a systematic treatment in Dunford and Schwartz (1958, 1963,
1971). This property also forms a preliminary topic of the book by ColojoarË˜a
and C. FoiaÂ¸s (1968). The fundamental role of SVEP in local spectral theory is
more clearly explained in the recent monograph Laursen and Neumann (2000).
The characterizations of the analytical core and the quasi-nilpotent part of
an operator given in Theorem 27.2.1 and Theorem 27.2.2 are due to VrbovÂ´a
(1973) and Mbekhta (1987, 1990); see also Proposition 2.2 of Laursen (1992).
ThelocalizedSVEPatapointhasbeenintroducedbyFinch(1975),whoshowed
that a surjective operator T has the SVEP at 0 precisely when it is injective.
The characterization of SVEP at a single point Î»0 given in Theorem 27.2.3 is
taken from Aiena and Monsalve (2000).
Except for Corollary 27.2.5, which is due to Mbekhta (1990), the source
of the results of the second section is essentially that of Aiena, Miller and
Neumann (2001) and Aiena, Colasante and Gonzalez (2002). In particular,
Example 27.2.6 and the local spectral mapping result of Theorem 27.2.10 are
taken from Aiena, Miller and Neumann (2001). In this paper the reader may ï¬nd
applications to isometries, analytic Toeplitz operators, invertible composition
operators on Hardy spaces, and weighted shifts.

28
SVEP for semi-Fredholm operators
In Chapter 27 we established many conditions which imply SVEP at a point.
The main goal of this chapter is to show that all these implications become
equivalences for an important class of operators, the class of all semi-Fredholm
operators. It will be also shown that, for these operators, SVEP at a point
Î»0 âˆˆC is equivalent to the ï¬niteness of two important quantities, the ascent and
the descent of the operator Î»0I âˆ’T . These equivalences also provide useful
information on the ï¬ne structure of the spectrum. In particular, we shall show
that many spectra originating from Fredholm theory coincide whenever T or
T â€² have SVEP.
28.1 Ascent, descent, and semi-Fredholm operators
Let us recall the deï¬nition of some classical quantities associated with an op-
erator. Given a linear operator T on a vector space X, it is easy to see that
ker T n âŠ†ker T n+1 and T n+1(X) âŠ†T n(X) for every n âˆˆN.
Deï¬nition 28.1.1
The ascent of T is the smallest positive integer p = p(T ),
whenever it exists, such that ker T p = ker T p+1. If such p does not exist, set
p = âˆ. Analogously, the descent of T is deï¬ned to be the smallest integer
q = q(T ), whenever it exists, such that T q+1(X) = T q(X). If such q does not
exist, set q = âˆ. If both p(T ) and q(T ) are ï¬nite, then T has ï¬nite chains.
Note that p(T ) = 0 means that T is injective and q(T ) = 0 means that T is
surjective. Clearly, T has ï¬nite ascent if and only if N âˆ(T ) = ker T k for some
k âˆˆN, and, analogously, T has ï¬nite descent if and only if T âˆ(X) = T k(X)
for some k âˆˆN.
The relevant fact is that the ï¬niteness of the ascent p(T ) may be expressed
by means of some intersection properties between kernels and ranges of the
iterates of T .
298

28
SVEP for semi-Fredholm operators
299
Lemma 28.1.2
Let T be a linear operator on a vector space X, and let

Theorem 28.1.3

28.2 Fredholm operators
Deï¬nition 28.2.1
A bounded operator T âˆˆB(X), where X is a Banach space,
is upper semi-Fredholm if Î±(T ) < âˆand T (X) is closed. The operator T in
B(X) is lower semi-Fredholm if Î²(T ) < âˆ. Denote by +(X) the class of all
upper semi-Fredholm operators, and by âˆ’(X) the class of all lower semi-
Fredholm operators. The class (X) of all Fredholm operators is deï¬ned by
(X) := +(X) âˆ©âˆ’(X), while the class of all semi-Fredholm operators is
deï¬ned by Â±(X) := +(X) âˆªâˆ’(X).
Note that, if Î²(T ) < âˆ, then T (X) is closed (see Chapter 26). If T âˆˆÂ±(X),
we can deï¬ne the index of T as above. Obviously, the index of a semi-Fredholm
operator is an integer or Â±âˆ.
The three sets +(X), âˆ’(X), and (X) are semi-groups, in the sense that,
if T, S belong to one of these sets, then also the products T S and ST lie in the
same set. Moreover, these three sets are each open, T âˆˆ+(X) if and only if
T â€² âˆˆâˆ’(Xâ€²), and dually, T âˆˆâˆ’(X) if and only if T â€² âˆˆ+(Xâ€²); the reader
can ï¬nd these classical results on Fredholm theory in Caradus, Pfaffenberger
and Yood (1974).

To explore SVEP at a point in the case of semi-Fredholm operators, we need
a decomposition property for these operators due to Kato (1958). We state this

Theorem 28.2.2
Let T âˆˆÂ±(X), where X is a Banach space. Then there
exist closed T -invariant subspaces M, N of X such that X = M âŠ•N, T | M
is semi-regular, T | N is nilpotent, and N is ï¬nite-dimensional.
For an arbitrary bounded operator T âˆˆB(X), a pair (M, N) of closed T -
invariant subspaces is called a Kato decomposition for T if the decompo-

dimensional. In the literature the operators which admit a Kato decomposition
are called essentially semi-regular; see MÂ¨uller (1994). A semi-regular operator
is obviously another example of an operator which admits a Kato decomp-

Fredholm T is semi-regular precisely when its jump j(T ) = 0; see Part IV,
Chapter 24 for the deï¬nition and also West (1987).
It is not difï¬cult to show that, if (M, N) is a Kato decomposition for T ,
then the pair (N âŠ¥, MâŠ¥) is a Kato decomposition for T â€². The proof of this fact
requires methods of standard duality theory (Exercise).

Remark 28.2.4
It should be noted that, if (M, N) is a Kato decomposition
for Î»0I âˆ’T , then T has SVEP at Î»0 if and only if T | M has SVEP at Î»0. In

Let Ïƒap(T ) be the approximate point spectrum of T and let Ïƒsu(T ) be the sur-

observed that T has SVEP at every point which is not a limit point of Ïƒ(T ).

Two other important spectra originating from the theory of Fredholm op-
erators are the Weyl spectrum and the Browder spectrum. The Weyl spectrum
Ïƒw(T ) of T âˆˆB(X) is deï¬ned to be the complement of those complex Î» âˆˆC

The next result extends to decomposable operators on Banach spaces some
classical results valid for normal operators on Hilbert spaces.
Theorem 28.2.16

T or T â€² has SVEP. Then Ïƒb(T ) = Ïƒw(T ). If both T and T â€² have SVEP, then
Ïƒs f (T ) = Ïƒe(T ) = Ïƒw(T ) = Ïƒb(T ) .
In particular, these equalities hold for every decomposable operator.
Proof Suppose ï¬rst that T has SVEP. By Proposition 28.2.15, T is a Fredholm
operator having index 0 if and only if T is a Rieszâ€“Schauder operator, and hence
Ïƒb(T ) = Ïƒw(T ). The same argument works also in the case that T â€² has SVEP
since T and T â€² are simultaneously Weyl or Browder, so that Ïƒw(T ) = Ïƒw(T â€²)
and Ïƒb(T ) = Ïƒb(T â€²). If both T and T â€² have SVEP then, from Corollary 28.2.7,
we easily obtain that Ïƒs f (T ) = Ïƒe(T ) = Ïƒb(T ).

28.4 Additional notes
Most of the material of this chapter is a sample of results of Aiena and
Monsalve (2000, 2001), Aiena, Colasante and Gonzalez (2001), and Aiena
and Rosas (2003). In these papers the results have been established in the more

28
SVEP for semi-Fredholm operators
315
general situation of Kato type operators, that is, operators on a Banach space for
which there exists a decomposition X = M âŠ•N, where M and N are closed
T -invariant subspaces, such that T | M is semi-regular and T | N is nilpotent.
Some of these results, in the special case of semi-Fredholm operators, may be
found in Finch (1975) and Schmoeger (1995). Note that some of the equiva-
lences of Proposition 28.2.6, Proposition 28.2.8, and Proposition 28.2.9 have
been established in West (1987) by using methods of Fredholm theory, without
involving local spectral theory.
The condition that p(Î»I âˆ’T ) < âˆfor all Î» âˆˆC has been investigated in
Laursen (1992) by different methods. In this paper the reader may ï¬nd some
interesting open problems. Finally, results very close to those established in the
corollaries of Theorem 28.2.10 may be found in Section 3.7 of Laursen and
Neumann (2000).
